**The play:** When buyers already code against a dominant API, mirror that exact interface so migrating to you is a base-URL-and-key change, then win on price and seed accounts with free credits. You turn the incumbent's lock-in into your funnel by making a trial a non-event.

**How [Baseten](https://baseten.co) did it** _(Reported)_ — Most developers already write against OpenAI's API, so switching providers normally means a code rewrite. Baseten built a library of 600+ open-source models (Llama, DeepSeek) deployable behind an endpoint in two clicks and exposed them through OpenAI-compatible APIs, so migrating means changing only the base URL and key. They seed new accounts with free credits and price tokens typically over 50% below comparable access (e.g. DeepSeek V3.1 at $0.50 in / $1.50 out per 1M tokens).

**Result:** Models deployable in two clicks via OpenAI-compatible endpoints at rates typically 50%+ lower than comparable access, with free credits removing onboarding friction for the large base of developers already fluent in the OpenAI SDK.
**The cost:** Mirroring OpenAI's interface so switching is a one-line base-URL change makes Baseten just as trivially swappable for the next cheaper clone, and pricing 50%+ below comparable access sets a thin-margin commodity floor in a business where GPU costs dominate.
**When it works:** Works when there is a widely-adopted incumbent API surface you can legally and faithfully clone, and you can deliver comparable output at a materially lower price.
**Source:** [Sacra company profile ('one-click access to open-source models such as Llama and DeepSeek via OpenAI-compatible endpoints' at rates 'typically over 50% lower'); Baseten model library and docs (pricing e.g. DeepSeek V3.1 $0.50 in / $1.50 out per 1M tokens)](https://sacra.com/c/baseten/)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `docs-as-marketing` `free-tier` `templates-starters` `usage-based-pricing`

---

<a id="recipe-128"></a>

#### Make switching cost one line of YAML

**The play:** When you replace an incumbent users invoke through a config string, be bit-for-bit compatible with its interface and environment, identical base images, tags, pre-installed deps, so adoption is editing one line on a branch instead of a migration project. Drive the cost of trying you to zero.

**How [Blacksmith](https://blacksmith.sh) did it** _(Verified)_ — Migrating CI runners normally means re-architecting pipelines, so teams stay on slow GitHub-hosted runners by inertia. Blacksmith built a true drop-in: customers swap `runs-on: ubuntu-latest` for a Blacksmith tag and change nothing else, because its runners boot off the identical images and pre-installed dependencies inside Firecracker microVMs (under 3 seconds, on gaming-grade CPUs). Near-zero migration cost let teams feel the 2x speedup and ~50% cost cut within minutes, fueling 4 people to $3.5M ARR and 700+ paying customers in under a year.

**Result:** One-line migration, value in minutes. Mintlify cut CI from 8 to 4 minutes and saved ~50% on CI spend. $3.5M ARR / 700+ paying customers (1,000+ orgs) in under a year.
**The cost:** Bit-for-bit compatibility means Blacksmith must perpetually mirror GitHub's runner images, tags, and environment, so any change GitHub ships can silently break the drop-in promise, and the same one-line reversibility that makes adoption frictionless makes ripping Blacksmith back out just as frictionless, leaving zero switching-cost lock-in.
**When it works:** Works when you replace a component the user already wires in by a standard identifier (a runner tag, a registry URL, an endpoint) and you can fully match its surface.
**Source:** [Blacksmith Series A press release ('migrate with just one line of code and start shipping faster within minutes'); Mintlify customer story, co-founder Hahnbee Lee: 'It really was just a few button clicks to get Blacksmith to work.' (https://www.blacksmith.sh/customer-stories/mintlify); Firecracker <3s boot / gaming-CPU mechanism per Blacksmith product page (https://www.blacksmith.sh/github-action-runners)](https://www.prnewswire.com/news-releases/blacksmith-raises-10m-to-unblock-ai-development-with-fast-ci-for-github-actions-302559870.html)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `default-on` `drop-in-replacement` `onboarding-redesign`

---

<a id="recipe-129"></a>

#### Show the result before you ask for anything

**The play:** Move every gate, login, payment, and config, to after the user has seen their first real result, and build the entire first screen around the one action that produces it. The cost of trying you drops to zero, and you only ask for commitment once the value is already on screen.

**How [Bolt.new](https://bolt.new) did it** _(Verified)_ — Bolt.new's landing page is a single text box: no login, no credit card, no marketing copy, no signup wall. A visitor types the app they want, hits enter, and a running web application appears in roughly 30 seconds, only hitting the signup/paywall after the value is already on screen. Eric Simons framed this as the differentiator against tools where 'there's a bajillion things going on.' Because 60-70% of users are non-developers, removing every barrier before the aha moment is what converted viral traffic into the fastest activation-to-revenue curve on record.

**Result:** Time-to-first-app under ~60 seconds with zero pre-value friction; $0 to $4M ARR in 4 weeks, 60-70% of users non-developers.
**The cost:** Handing real, compute-heavy results to anonymous visitors before any commitment means you pay the full LLM and WebContainers cost of every tire-kicker and abuser who never converts, which is why gross margins sit at only ~40%.
**When it works:** Works when you can deliver genuine first value without knowing who the user is and your compute/abuse costs survive anonymous usage; fails when value requires account data or anonymous use is too expensive to give away.
**Source:** [Eric Simons (co-founder/CEO) on The Peel podcast with Turner Novak](https://www.thespl.it/p/zero-to-20m-arr-in-two-months-inside)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `onboarding-redesign` `time-to-value`

---

<a id="recipe-130"></a>

#### If adopting you costs more than one edit, you lose

**The play:** When you replace something devs already run in prod, make the switch a single config line (the FROM line, one import, one env var) and ship a fully-featured variant next to the minimal one so nothing breaks on swap. Trying you should cost a single edit.

**How [Chainguard](https://chainguard.dev) did it** _(Reported)_ — Security tools usually fail because they force developers to change their build. Chainguard shipped hardened, distroless container images (only the app and runtime, no shell or package manager) that swap into an existing Dockerfile by editing only the FROM line, or by repointing a Helm chart, with no other rewrite. To kill the last friction, they also ship a 'dev variant' of nearly every image that keeps a shell and package manager so the swap is truly drop-in. Developers could adopt secure-by-default infrastructure inside their current pipeline, then standardize across the org.

**Result:** Drop-in adoption drove a 5X year-over-year customer-base increase and 175%+ ARR growth in the first six months of the fiscal year, contributing to ~7x ARR growth to $40M and 600+ customers including Anduril, Snowflake, HPE, and Canva.
**The cost:** A one-line FROM swap that adds no proprietary surface cuts both ways: the same edit that wins a customer lets them revert just as cheaply, so you compete on a commoditized layer and must re-earn the value with every CVE delta rather than locking anyone in; the 'dev' variant that ships a shell and package manager to guarantee the swap also reintroduces the attack surface the hardened image existed to remove.
**When it works:** Works when teams already run the component in production and the value (here, fewer CVEs) shows up without any behavior change.
**Source:** [Chainguard Series C press release (PR Newswire), corroborated by Chainguard Academy docs on dev variants and Dockerfile FROM-line swap](https://www.prnewswire.com/news-releases/software-security-leader-chainguard-raises-140-million-in-series-c-funding-to-secure-the-next-frontier-of-ai-workloads-302206133.html)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `default-on` `land-and-expand` `open-source` `time-to-value`

---

<a id="recipe-131"></a>

#### Fork the incumbent so switching to you costs nothing

**The play:** Against a sticky incumbent, build on its forkable foundation and ship a one-click import of the user's existing extensions, themes, and keybindings on first launch. Trial then costs them zero setup, and you compete on your new value instead of their accumulated muscle memory.

**How [Cursor](https://cursor.com) did it** _(Verified)_ — Instead of building a new IDE from scratch, Cursor forked VS Code so it inherited the entire extension and theme ecosystem. On first launch it detects an existing VS Code install and offers a one-click import that transfers extensions, themes, keybindings, and all settings. Switching cost a developer almost nothing, so Cursor competed on its AI features instead of fighting years of muscle memory.

**Result:** Developers keep their full VS Code environment on switch; the fork let Cursor inherit the VS Code ecosystem while owning the AI layer, a key enabler of its zero-marketing, word-of-mouth growth to $1B+ ARR.
**The cost:** Forking VS Code chains you to Microsoft's upstream: you carry the permanent cost of rebasing your AI layer onto their releases and you depend on a competitor's terms for the proprietary extension marketplace, which they can restrict against forks at any time.
**When it works:** Works when switching pain comes mostly from setup and muscle memory, with little lock-in from data or contracts.
**Source:** [Cursor official docs: "VS Code Import" one-click import of extensions, themes, settings, and keybindings](https://cursor.com/docs/configuration/migrations/vscode)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `default-on` `migration` `onboarding-redesign` `open-source`

---

<a id="recipe-132"></a>

#### Win the first session with one on-by-default wow

**The play:** Engineer a single wedge feature that delivers a visible payoff within seconds of install, ship it on by default with zero configuration, and let that one moment drive trial-to-habit and word-of-mouth. For Cursor it was Tab predicting your next edit the instant you typed.

**How [Cursor](https://cursor.com) did it** _(Verified)_ — Cursor's wedge was Tab, a custom model that predicts your next edit (not just the next token) and fires the instant you type, with no setup or prompting. By Cursor's own account Tab "opened the first era of AI-assisted coding" and gave "significant leverage for nearly two years." The visceral, on-by-default wow moment turned trials into daily habits and made developers evangelize the tool unprompted.

**Result:** Tab was the PMF trigger and the primary feature through early 2025 (2.5x more users than agents as of March 2025); it anchored Cursor's rise to the fastest SaaS company ever to $100M ARR (12 months).
**The cost:** By Cursor's own account Tab gave leverage for only "nearly two years," so a single copyable wedge feature buys a finite head start that erodes the moment rivals replicate the trick and the surprise becomes table stakes.
**When it works:** Works when a genuinely surprising result is reachable without setup, data, or a learning curve to trigger it.
**Source:** [Cursor official blog, "The third era of AI software development"](https://cursor.com/blog/third-era)
**Tags:** `default-on` `developer-advocacy` `wedge` `word-of-mouth`

---

<a id="recipe-133"></a>

#### Don't build the integration long tail yourself

**The play:** Make connecting the user's existing stack a click-to-see-data step, then open an integration SDK so technology partners build the long tail; catalog breadth scales past 1,000 integrations without you hiring for each one.

**How [Datadog](https://datadoghq.com) did it** _(Verified)_ — Datadog made turnkey integrations the wedge: a new user connects AWS, containers, databases, and SaaS in minutes and immediately sees correlated signals, so the platform lands fast and pulls in more of the org's tooling. The non-obvious move is that Datadog opened the catalog to outside technology partners rather than building everything in-house, so breadth scales past 1,000 integrations without Datadog hiring for each one. Integrations ship default-on, becoming the land-and-expand surface.

**Result:** Passed 1,000 integrations with roughly a third built by registered technology partners; organizations monitoring hosts grew 20% YoY and 99% of container orgs rely on container integrations.
**The cost:** You hand the quality, maintenance, and breakage of integrations you depend on to outside partners, taking on the ongoing burden of running an SDK and partner program plus reputational exposure when a third-party integration silently fails.
**When it works:** Works when your product sits atop an ecosystem of tools users already run and value comes from correlating across them; less relevant for standalone products with no surrounding stack to connect.
**Source:** [Datadog engineering blog, 'Scaling Datadog observability: 1,000 integrations and counting'](https://www.datadoghq.com/blog/1k-integrations-milestone/)
**Tags:** `default-on` `integrations-marketplace` `land-and-expand`

---

<a id="recipe-134"></a>

#### Ship your free tier into the surface users already keep open

**The play:** Embed the free tier inside the tool your users live in and gate it with the account they already have, so trying you costs one sign-in: no install, no new credential. Owning the daily surface drops your whole installed base into the funnel and beats any standalone signup page.

**How [GitHub Copilot](https://github.com/features/copilot) did it** _(Verified)_ — GitHub already owned the most-used editor (VS Code) and the largest developer account base. On December 18, 2024 it shipped Copilot Free integrated directly into VS Code: any developer got 2,000 completions and 50 chat messages a month just by signing in with their existing GitHub account, no credit card and nothing new to install. The free tier lived where developers already worked, so activation friction collapsed to a single sign-in and the entire installed base was dropped into a paid-conversion funnel.

**Result:** Free tier shipped default-on in VS Code alongside the 150M-developer milestone; the free plan became top-of-funnel for ~4.7M paid Copilot subscribers (Microsoft FY26 Q2, Jan 2026).
**The cost:** Dropping your entire installed base into a generous free tier means you carry the marginal inference cost of millions of non-payers indefinitely, and most of them stay free, so the ~18% conversion has to subsidize the other ~82% you can never bill.
**When it works:** Works when you already control or can natively embed into a high-frequency surface your target users keep open every day.
**Source:** [GitHub Blog, 'Announcing 150M developers and a new free tier for GitHub Copilot in VS Code' (Dec 18, 2024)](https://github.blog/news-insights/product-news/github-copilot-in-vscode-free/)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `default-on` `free-tier` `freemium`

---

<a id="recipe-135"></a>

#### Embed in your buyer's office until their vocabulary is yours

**The play:** If you sell to a buyer whose daily work you don't live, work free inside a few early accounts one standing day a week for months until their workflows and words are yours. You feel the pain in real time, which beats surveys and advisory calls and shapes both the product and the positioning that unblocks sales.

**How [Huntress](https://huntress.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — Not understanding the MSP buyer he was selling to, Kyle Hanslovan offered to work for free one day a week inside early MSP partners' offices, and did it for roughly the first two and a half years. Living their actual workflows surfaced exact pain points and language no case study or consultant could provide, which directly shaped both the product and the channel-first GTM.

**Result:** Produced the buyer intimacy and messaging that underpinned the MSP channel model and growth past $100M ARR.
**The cost:** Standing a day a week inside partner offices for roughly two and a half years is unscalable founder time that doesn't compound, spent on a handful of accounts while everything else the founder could be building waits.
**When it works:** When your buyer's workflow is opaque to outsiders and getting the positioning wrong is the main thing blocking sales.
**Source:** [Kyle Hanslovan (CEO), SaaS Club podcast ep. 429; corroborated on Frontlines.io](https://saasclub.io/podcast/huntress-kyle-hanslovan-429/)
**Tags:** `design-partners` `founder-led-sales` `positioning`

---

<a id="recipe-136"></a>

#### Make the entry cost the buyer nothing, make the upgrade obvious

**The play:** When your full product demands a heavyweight switch, ship a near-zero-install entry (extension, scoped access, no agent or MDM) that proves value on the user's own device, and hold the deeper capabilities (DLP, app isolation, advanced identity) back as the explicit reason to expand.

**How [Island](https://island.io) did it** _(Reported)_ — Asking an org to swap its default browser is a deal-killing ask. Island made the entry near-zero-friction: contractors and BYOD users install the browser (or a Chrome/Edge/Safari/Firefox extension) on any personal device, authenticate, and get scoped access to only their apps, with no MDM, no device imaging, and no agent to push. Risk-averse buyers start with this light footprint to get visibility and basic control inside existing workflows, then upgrade to the full browser once they hit use cases (sensitive-data DLP, app isolation, advanced identity) that demand it.

**Result:** Light-footprint entry (no agent, no MDM, any device) let a global hospitality company make Island the access layer for 15,000 employees and partners and retire Citrix plus a legacy password vault; deployments run from days to a couple of weeks.
**The cost:** Making entry near-free with a scoped extension means Island must build and support two products at once (a thin extension layer and a full Chromium browser) and bets on advanced use cases pulling accounts to the heavy switch, so accounts that never feel that pain stall on the light tier, which is also trivially copyable by competitors.
**When it works:** Works when a stripped-down footprint still delivers real value on day one; fails if the light version is too weak to prove anything.
**Source:** [Island third-party contractor and BYOD solution pages (no agent/MDM, install on any device); two-tier model from Island enterprise-browser-extensions blog](https://www.island.io/solutions/3rd-party-contractors)
**Tags:** `land-and-expand` `onboarding-redesign`

---

<a id="recipe-137"></a>

#### Be the feature you haven't built yet

**The play:** Hand-migrate and copyedit each early customer's docs yourself, positioning as their technical writer so a thin product wins on service. The steps you keep repeating by hand are the literal spec for self-serve onboarding, so instrument what you do and productize it.

**How [Mintlify](https://mintlify.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — Switching docs platforms is high-friction, so the founders absorbed it: they spent hours-to-days per customer manually migrating existing docs and even copyediting the customer's content, positioning themselves as the company's technical writers to mask an early product. That white-glove service won the first 10 customers (starting with YC batchmate Hyperbeam, plus a roommate and founder friends), and watching what they did by hand became the spec for the self-serve onboarding they later productized.

**Result:** Landed first 10 customers and removed switching friction; the manual service became unsustainable past ~1,000 customers and was converted into self-serve onboarding.
**The cost:** Posing as the customer's technical writer hides a thin product behind founder labor that scales one-to-one with customers, so the service became unsustainable past ~1,000 accounts and forced a scramble to productize before the manual work buried the team.
**When it works:** Works in the first ~100 customers when the product is still rough.
**Source:** [Mintlify blog, 'How we grew Mintlify by doing things that don't scale'; first-10-customers detail from Hahnbee Lee LinkedIn](https://www.mintlify.com/blog/things-that-do-not-scale)
**Tags:** `design-partners` `founder-led-sales` `onboarding-redesign`

---

<a id="recipe-138"></a>

#### The friction you cut at prototype is the revenue you keep at scale

**The play:** Strip the credit card, the sales call, and the setup wizard so a developer hits a running instance in minutes, and pair it with usage-based pricing so a successful prototype expands into a large workload with no renegotiation. Track time-to-running-instance as your activation metric.

**How [MongoDB](https://mongodb.com) did it** _(Verified)_ — When CEO Dev Ittycheria saw developers already self-deploying MongoDB in the cloud, the company launched Atlas (June 2016) with a free M0 cluster that requires no payment, no credit card, and stands up in ~3 minutes. Removing all DevOps and procurement friction for prototypes meant workloads started on the free tier and expanded into production naturally, with the same self-serve accounts later growing into seven-figure enterprise spend.

**Result:** Atlas grew to ~73% of total revenue by FY2026 ($1B+ run-rate); ~88% of customers are self-serve, and 25% of $1M+ customers originated self-serve, reaching $1M ARR ~15% faster than direct-sales-sourced accounts.
**The cost:** Removing the credit card and signup friction means you absorb the infrastructure cost of a large non-paying M0 population with no revenue or qualification signal at signup, betting entirely on later expansion (the ~0.04% downloads-to-customer proxy shows how noisy that top of funnel is).
**When it works:** Works when success at prototype scale naturally drives much larger production usage.
**Source:** [MongoDB Atlas docs (free M0 cluster, ~3 min, no credit card); FY2025/FY2026 filings (Atlas ~73% of revenue; 53,100+ Atlas vs 7,500+ direct-sales customers); SaaStr ('5 Interesting Learnings from MongoDB at $2.4B ARR': 25% of $1M+ customers self-serve-originated, reaching $1M 15% faster).](https://www.mongodb.com/docs/atlas/tutorial/deploy-free-tier-cluster/)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `free-tier` `freemium` `land-and-expand` `usage-based-pricing`

---

<a id="recipe-139"></a>

#### Against identical clones, the one with zero setup wins

**The play:** Ship a single zero-dependency binary with a built-in auto-updater so first value lands in one command. When competitors are functionally identical, the one that goes from download to working in seconds takes the distribution.

**How [ngrok](https://ngrok.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — Alan Shreve built ngrok as a single self-contained Go binary you drop into your path and run, with a built-in auto-updater and zero config, so a developer goes from download to a working public tunnel in seconds. That friction-free first run made ngrok stick in daily testing and demo workflows where competing localtunnel tools (with runtimes, dependencies, or setup steps) did not. Shreve attributes ngrok taking off over functionally similar clones to obsessing over the first-run experience.

**Result:** Near-instant time-to-first-tunnel drove broad daily adoption; Shreve noted 'most casual users never really break out of that free tier,' keeping the top of funnel huge and self-serve.
**The cost:** Optimizing the first run so anyone gets a working tunnel in seconds means most casual users, by Shreve's own account, never break out of the free tier, so you fund and support an enormous non-paying top of funnel and inherit a chronically low free-to-paid conversion (~0.5%) as the price of frictionless adoption.
**When it works:** Works for developer tools installed locally where setup friction (runtimes, dependencies, config) is the main thing standing between download and first value.
**Source:** [Alan Shreve, Changelog Interviews #210: 'just a single Go binary, you drop it into your path and it runs'; built-in auto-update; 'most casual users never really break out of that free tier.'](https://changelog.com/podcast/210)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `developer-advocacy` `free-tier` `onboarding-redesign`

---

<a id="recipe-140"></a>

#### Let the buyer hold the throttle on autonomy

**The play:** When your product takes irreversible actions on someone's production systems, ship it defaulting to watch-only and gate each escalation (watch to suggest to act) behind an explicit per-stage opt-in. Take read-write access up front so raising the mode never means re-integrating, and let trust accrue on the buyer's timeline.

**How [Sedai](https://sedai.io) did it** _(Verified)_ — Selling an 'autonomous' platform that takes real actions in production terrifies enterprise buyers, so saying yes feels like betting the cluster on day one. Sedai removed that risk by making the default mode observe-only: new accounts start in Datapilot (analyzes monitoring data, changes nothing), graduate to Copilot (proposes changes the team approves one by one), and only flip to Autopilot once trust is earned. Crucially, the system makes zero changes until the customer explicitly raises the mode, so the buyer controls the throttle and can adopt without risk.

**Result:** Fortune 500 firms (Palo Alto Networks, Experian, KnowBe4) ramped to full autonomy; 25M+ production actions executed with zero customer-reported incidents. Onboarding asks for read-write access up front so upgrading modes never requires re-integration.
**The cost:** Defaulting to a mode that changes nothing and requiring an explicit per-stage opt-in means the product can stall indefinitely in low-value watch mode, deferring both customer value and your revenue until the buyer chooses, on their own timeline, to grant the autonomy you actually monetize.
**When it works:** Works when the buyer's fear is loss-of-control over production and your value compounds with autonomy.
**Source:** [Sedai official docs, 'Understanding Operation Modes' (Datapilot/Copilot/Autopilot, Datapilot is the default); corroborated by Sedai Series B blog (June 16, 2025)](https://docs.sedai.io/get-started/onboarding/readme/understanding-operation-modes)
**Tags:** `land-and-expand` `onboarding-redesign` `transparency`

---

<a id="recipe-141"></a>

#### Turn your steepest learning curve into your viral loop

**The play:** Put your hardest authoring step in a zero-install browser sandbox and make every output a shareable, embeddable, live-running link; the artifacts users create to learn the product become demos that recruit the next users.

**How [Semgrep](https://semgrep.dev) did it** _(Reported)_ — Writing static-analysis rules has a steep learning curve that blocks adoption. Semgrep built a free, no-install browser Playground where anyone writes and tests a rule against sample code and sees matches instantly, re-scanning on every keystroke (Turbo Mode). Users save and share rules via a permalink, and any rule embeds as a live, runnable widget (semgrep.dev/embed/editor?snippet=ID). Every shared or embedded rule link is a working demo that pulls new developers in, so onboarding doubles as a viral loop.

**Result:** Zero-install, shareable rule editor (free for OSS and commercial use) that compresses time-to-first-rule to minutes and turns each shared/embedded rule into a runnable demo.
**The cost:** Giving away a free, no-install Playground that runs the hardest capability forever surrenders the authoring experience as a paid surface and lets users and rivals extract value without ever entering the funnel.
**When it works:** Works when your product has a steep authoring or configuration step and its outputs are small, self-contained, and interesting enough that people want to share them.
**Source:** [Semgrep Playground official docs; sharing/embed mechanism per Semgrep docs (semgrep.dev/embed/editor?snippet=)](https://semgrep.dev/docs/playground/)
**Tags:** `developer-advocacy` `docs-as-marketing` `free-tier` `viral-loop`

---

<a id="recipe-142"></a>

#### Leave onboarding manual so the founder personally touches every early signup

**The play:** In the first weeks, don't automate activation. Have the founder approve each account by hand so you get a direct line to early adopters that automation would silence, surfacing which features actually matter before you build them. Rip it out the moment volume outgrows one person.

**How [Tailscale](https://tailscale.com) did it** _(Verified)_ — When Tailscale's early HN post hit, it produced hundreds of signups the first night. Instead of fully automating, Pennarun stayed up personally activating accounts, opening a direct line to the earliest adopters and surfacing feedback that shaped product priorities. They kept the same human tone at the conversion edge, enforcing the device limit with a polite 'would you mind paying us' email rather than a technical block.

**Result:** Hundreds of users in the first night from one HN post; founder-led activation produced direct early-adopter feedback that shaped the roadmap and converted goodwill into early paying customers.
**The cost:** Founder-led activation bottlenecks onboarding on one person's time and caps how fast you can absorb signups, so the feedback channel only works at tiny volume and must be torn out the moment growth outpaces a single human.
**When it works:** Works at the earliest stage when signup volume is still small enough for a founder to handle by hand.
**Source:** [CEO Avery Pennarun, Stratechery interview (2025)](https://stratechery.com/2025/an-interview-with-tailscale-co-founder-and-ceo-avery-pennarun/)
**Tags:** `community-led` `founder-led-sales`

---

<a id="recipe-143"></a>

#### Your head of product should personally DM hundreds of free users a month

**The play:** Put a founder or head of product on direct, high-volume DMs to your active OSS users in a tight window, asking exactly how they run you in production. The more senior the user base, the more each conversation both corrects your roadmap and pre-qualifies your best enterprise accounts.

**How [Temporal](https://temporal.io) did it** _(Reported)_ — Temporal treated its open-source Slack as a research and relationship channel, not a support queue. Head of Product Ryland Goldstein (employee #5) personally messaged 600 users in a single month to learn how they actually ran Temporal in production. The community skewed senior, engineers from top-tier companies running mission-critical systems, so these conversations directly shaped the roadmap and seeded the highest-quality enterprise Cloud accounts, while Goldstein layered on transparency reports and detailed release notes to keep the loop warm.

**Result:** Direct outreach to 600 Slack users in one month fed a roadmap and community that grew 25-50% MoM; 50K+ new OSS developers and 300+ new customers joined in the year to Feb 2022.
**The cost:** This is senior-person manual labor that doesn't scale, making the head of product the bottleneck, so the channel breaks the moment the community outgrows what one person can personally reach.
**When it works:** Works when your free/OSS users are senior engineers deploying you for real.
**Source:** [Contrary Research, citing Temporal Head of Product Ryland Goldstein](https://research.contrary.com/company/temporal-technologies)
**Tags:** `community-led` `design-partners` `founder-led-sales` `slack-community`

---

<a id="recipe-144"></a>

#### Kill the blank canvas: make the first action a clone of a working flow

**The play:** Replace the empty builder with a library of one-click-importable templates covering your top real-world use cases (Tines shipped 100+, grew to 1,000+). A new user's first move is cloning a working flow and editing it.

**How [Tines](https://tines.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — New SOAR users stall because building a first automation from scratch is slow and intimidating, so they never reach value. Tines launched a Story Library of 100+ ready-made, one-click-import workflows (ServiceNow tickets, Active Directory access removal, VirusTotal scans) that users clone and customize instead of starting blank. The library became the default first stop for builders and grew to 1,000+ workflows co-built by customers and partners; for at least one enterprise customer, onboarding a process dropped from one to two months on Splunk to a single day on Tines.

**Result:** Library grew from 100+ at launch to 1,000+ workflows; one enterprise cut process onboarding from 1-2 months (Splunk) to 1 day on Tines.
**The cost:** Owning a 1,000+ template library is an open-ended maintenance liability where any upstream API or integration change can silently break clones, and one-click imports can anchor users to your prebuilt patterns instead of learning the builder deeply enough to expand on their own.
**When it works:** Works when your product is a flexible builder where the blank start is the main friction and common use cases are predictable enough to pre-template.
**Source:** [Tines blog 'Introducing the Story Library'; Tines onboarding/library blog posts (Splunk-to-Tines onboarding time)](https://www.tines.com/blog/introducing-the-tines-story-library/)
**Tags:** `community-led` `docs-as-marketing` `integrations-marketplace` `onboarding-redesign` `templates-starters`

---

<a id="recipe-145"></a>

#### Make the phone ring before you ask for a credit card

**The play:** Engineer the first action to produce a real, externally visible result, an actual SMS arriving or a phone ringing in minutes from copy-paste code, and hand out trial credits so no card is needed. Push every gate that doesn't block that first result, billing, compliance, card capture, all the way to production. The aha earns the signup.

**How [Twilio](https://twilio.com) did it** _(Verified)_ — VCs pushed Jeff Lawson to ship a finished app; he shipped a raw API instead and built onboarding around one magical moment: a few lines of code that make a real phone ring or send a real SMS in minutes. New accounts got $15 in trial credits and no credit card, and Twilio deliberately deferred the credit card and compliance steps until a developer was ready to launch in production. That barrier-free quick win converted curious coders into paying accounts at scale.

**Result:** Over 900,000 registered developer accounts and 90,000+ paying customer accounts as of March 31, 2016 (S-1); roughly 10% free-to-paid.
**The cost:** Deferring the credit card and compliance gate to production and handing out free credits means you bankroll the support, infrastructure, and abuse-surface of a huge unpaid majority: roughly 90% of those 900,000+ registered accounts never became paying customers, so you carry the cost of the demos that never convert.
**When it works:** Works when your product has a single demonstrable 'it actually worked' moment a developer can reach unaided.
**Source:** [Twilio S-1 (SEC, June 2016); onboarding detail from Twilio engineering blog and McKinsey interview with CEO Jeff Lawson](https://www.sec.gov/Archives/edgar/data/1447669/000104746916013448/a2227414zs-1.htm)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `time-to-value`

---

<a id="recipe-146"></a>

#### Evaluate locally so integration costs zero latency and zero privacy review

**The play:** Ship SDKs that cache all config in local memory and evaluate as a function on local state: nanosecond answers, no per-call round trip, no user data leaving the customer. That kills the two objections that stall first integration, performance tax and a privacy review, so a developer in any stack can drop it in unblocked.

**How [Unleash](https://getunleash.io) did it** _(Reported)_ — Unleash ships 12 official plus 10+ community SDKs, but the breadth isn't the real edge: the architecture is. Each SDK caches all flag config in local memory and evaluates flags as a function on local state, returning answers in nanoseconds with no per-evaluation network call. An optional Edge layer fans out to thousands of SDK connections without adding load to the Unleash server, and frontend evaluation happens without leaking user data back to Unleash. That combination removes the two objections that kill feature-flag adoption: latency/scaling cost and sending user data to a vendor, so a developer in nearly any stack can drop it in safely.

**Result:** 40M+ total downloads (18M+ via Docker), 13,300+ GitHub stars, 200+ contributors, the largest open-source feature-flag project on GitHub.
**The cost:** Because SDKs evaluate flags client-side off cached config with no per-evaluation call back to you, you forfeit the per-evaluation telemetry that would underpin usage-based pricing and product insight, and you commit to maintaining 12-plus official SDKs across every stack in perpetuity.
**When it works:** Works for tools whose decisions can be computed client-side from cached config.
**Source:** [Download/star counts per Unleash Series B blog and BusinessWire release (March 2026); local in-memory evaluation, nanosecond latency, and privacy-by-design architecture per getunleash.io/blog/our-unique-architecture and docs.getunleash.io](https://www.getunleash.io/blog/our-unique-architecture)
**Tags:** `open-source`

---

<a id="recipe-147"></a>

#### Deliver the outcome by hand, then hand it to customer two unchanged

**The play:** Run your product's outcome manually for the first customer, then hand the exact same artifact (spreadsheet, checklist, report) to a second customer in the same category with zero changes. If it holds unmodified, the problem generalizes and you now know precisely which steps to automate first.

**How [Vanta](https://vanta.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — Before building software, Christina Cacioppo ran SOC 2 readiness as a manual consulting service: a color-coded gap-assessment spreadsheet she filled in by hand, even setting up desks inside Segment's office for weeks reading dozens of real SOC 2 reports. She then handed the exact same spreadsheet to the next customer (Front) without modification, secretly testing whether Segment's controls and rules generalized. They did. Only after the spreadsheet repeatedly delivered value did the team write code, starting with a bare form where customers entered AWS credentials and the team pulled data and wrote reports by hand.

**Result:** Validated PMF with a concierge MVP, reached 600+ customers and stayed cash-flow break-even for ~3 years, crossing $10M ARR before raising a Series A, with no real website or marketing team.
**The cost:** Hand-delivering SOC 2 reports as a consulting service caps you at the throughput of a few experts reading reports at customer desks, so the company ran cash-flow break-even for roughly three years and crossed only $10M ARR before raising, deferring the scale that automation and capital could have bought earlier.
**When it works:** Works when the job is a repeatable expert workflow whose rules are stable across companies.
**Source:** [First Round Review, 'Vanta's Path to Product-Market Fit'; corroborated by SaaStr, 'The Early Days: 5 Things Vanta Got Right'](https://review.firstround.com/vantas-path-to-product-market-fit/)
**Tags:** `design-partners` `founder-led-sales`

---

<a id="recipe-148"></a>

#### Run the demo inside the prospect's own live cloud environment

**The play:** Connect read-only to the prospect's cloud control plane via API during the call and surface a real, prioritized risk in their own environment inside 15 minutes; the no-install, no-production-touch wedge makes trying you free, and a live finding nobody knew about turns the champion into your internal seller before procurement starts.

**How [Wiz](https://wiz.io) did it** _(Reported)_ — Cybersecurity sales normally drag 12-18 months because agents must be installed across production before anyone sees value. Wiz connected agentlessly to a prospect's cloud control plane via read-only API during the call itself, surfacing real, prioritized risks in that customer's own environment within 10-15 minutes. The 'you've got to see this' reaction turned the security champion into an internal seller who showed leadership a concrete win, collapsing the cycle. Wiz productized this as a standing '15-minute live demo' series.

**Result:** Up and running in 10-15 minutes with first findings in ~30 minutes; helped reach ~45% of the Fortune 100 and the fastest $1M-to-$100M ARR in cybersecurity history (18 months).
**The cost:** Agentless connection only sees what the cloud control plane exposes through the API, so you trade deep runtime and in-memory threat detection for the no-install speed and must bolt on agents later to close the visibility gap.
**When it works:** Works when your architecture can pull real, meaningful data from the buyer's environment through a read-only API alone, with no agent install required.
**Source:** [Software Analyst Substack 'The Wiz Playbook'; corroborated by Wiz '15-minute live demo' series (wiz.io/events) and Wiz Academy evaluation FAQ](https://softwareanalyst.substack.com/p/the-wiz-playbook-how-they-dominated)
**Tags:** `founder-led-sales` `time-to-value`

---

### Conversion

<a id="recipe-149"></a>

#### Peg your free cap to the unit teams accumulate when they get serious

**The play:** Set the free ceiling on the unit usage actually grows by (distinct images here), low enough that a team trips it the moment they standardize, so the limit becomes your qualified-lead trigger. Keep entry zero-login so the cap qualifies buyers instead of blocking the top of funnel.

**How [Chainguard](https://chainguard.dev) did it** _(Reported)_ — Chainguard launched Catalog Starter: five free trusted container images of your choice per organization, drawn from a catalog of 2,200+ projects, plus ~50 images you can docker pull with no login at all. The cap is deliberate. A single team prototyping hits the five-image ceiling fast once they standardize, and the free tier is explicitly framed as the gateway to land-and-expand. The zero-login pull removes even the signup barrier, while the five-image limit creates a natural, usage-driven reason to talk to sales.

**Result:** Launched March 2026 as a free trial-to-production on-ramp positioned as the entry point to enterprise adoption (no conversion rate disclosed).
**The cost:** A deliberately tight five-image cap is friction by design, so it taxes honest evaluation as much as it qualifies leads: a team still proving the value can hit the ceiling and bounce to free public base images or a competitor before they ever feel committed enough to buy.
**When it works:** When usage scales by a countable unit you can meter, and hitting the cap correlates with a team being serious enough to buy rather than just kicking tires.
**Source:** [Chainguard official announcement, March 17 2026](https://www.chainguard.dev/unchained/introducing-chainguard-catalog-starter)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `free-tier` `land-and-expand` `open-source` `templates-starters`

---

<a id="recipe-150"></a>

#### Put the thing the executive is liable for behind the paywall

**The play:** Take the part of your value an executive buyer is personally on the hook for and write it into an explicit SLA with hard numbers (7 days for critical CVEs, 1 day for KEV), then gate it. Free gets developers in; the contractual guarantee is what converts grassroots usage into budget.

**How [Chainguard](https://chainguard.dev) did it** _(Verified)_ — Free images get developers in, but enterprises pay for a promise. Chainguard turned 'low-CVE' from a vibe into a contract: a written remediation SLA of 7 days for critical CVEs, 14 days for high/medium/low, and 1 day for any CISA-listed Known Exploited Vulnerability, with the image guaranteed to be zero-CVE when you build with it. That contractual guarantee is what a CISO buys and what justifies the price over free base images, letting developer-led grassroots adoption convert into top-down enterprise purchases.

**Result:** The paid catalog (2,000+ images with version pinning and the CVE remediation SLA) underpins a land-and-expand motion reaching 600+ paying customers and a $3.5B valuation; the free tier of ~50 unauthenticated images is the on-ramp.
**The cost:** Turning 'low-CVE' into a written SLA (7 days critical, 1 day for KEV) converts a marketing claim into a perpetual operational liability: you must staff and sustain that remediation cadence across 2,000+ images indefinitely, and every miss is now a contractual breach you are financially and reputationally on the hook for.
**When it works:** When your buyer carries compliance or liability risk and a measurable guarantee (remediation time, uptime) is something they currently have to do manually and cannot easily self-promise.
**Source:** [Chainguard published CVE SLA policy; KEV one-day SLA per Chainguard Unchained blog](https://www.chainguard.dev/legal/cve-policy)
**Tags:** `developer-advocacy` `founder-led-sales` `land-and-expand`

---

<a id="recipe-151"></a>

#### Gate the already-loved free tool behind a company-size line

**The play:** When a free tool is entrenched inside large orgs, draw the paywall on an objective size threshold (headcount or revenue) so individuals stay free and viral while companies that already depend on you are forced to convert. Bundle enterprise-only features (SSO, central management, compliance) onto that tier so the mandated spend reads as a justified upgrade.

**How [Docker](https://docker.com) did it** _(Verified)_ — Docker Desktop ran free across a 20M+ developer install base, including inside large enterprises that depended on it daily. In August 2021 Docker kept it free for individuals, small businesses, education, and open source, but required a paid subscription for any company with 250+ employees or over $10M revenue. Because developers were already hooked, this converted latent free usage into recurring revenue with no sales motion, and a new $21/month Business tier (SSO, centralized management, supply-chain security) gave enterprises the features to justify the line item.

**Result:** Docker publicly estimated the change would roughly double its paid subscriber base; it helped drive a ~30x increase in subscription revenue from 2020 to 2023 and 1M+ paid seats by 2024.
**The cost:** Putting a paywall on a previously-free, entrenched tool breaks faith with a loyal base and risks them ripping it out for free alternatives, a danger CEO Scott Johnston conceded in the announcement: 'There is always a non-zero risk' of users abandoning Docker Desktop.
**When it works:** Works when you have entrenched free usage inside large orgs that would be painful to rip out, and a defensible size threshold cleanly separates those companies from your individual users.
**Source:** [The Register coverage of Docker's 2021 subscription change, quoting CEO Scott Johnston; threshold confirmed by Docker pricing docs](https://www.theregister.com/software/2021/08/31/docker-desktop-no-longer-free-for-large-companies/983136)
**Tags:** `freemium` `pricing-change`

---

<a id="recipe-152"></a>

#### Charge the time-poor, give the time-rich everything free

**The play:** Split your tiers by who is budget-rich and time-poor. Hand hobbyists the full product free as permanent top-of-funnel, and reserve the bill for managed scale, compliance, and SLAs that only well-funded teams need and will pay to skip.

**How [Grafana Labs](https://grafana.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — Instead of feature-gating to squeeze every user, Grafana deliberately leaves ~90% of its users free forever, including home-lab and hobbyist setups. CEO Raj Dutt frames the paid product as built to 'monetize people who have more money than time': enterprises buy Cloud and Enterprise for managed scale, compliance, security, and SLAs, while the huge free base seeds brand loyalty and feeds inbound enterprise discovery. The bet is to grow the total pie and capture a thin slice of it rather than a fat slice of a small pie.

**Result:** Roughly 1% of users pay by design (about 7,000 customers against 35M+ users), yet that slice produced $400M+ ARR at 125% NRR.
**The cost:** Choosing to leave ~90% of users free forever means your entire business rides on converting a thin ~1% slice, so you absorb the cost of serving a 35M-user free base while a single segmentation miscalculation or a free tier generous enough to satisfy mid-market teams quietly caps the revenue you can ever collect.
**When it works:** Works when your free users generate distribution and enterprise pipeline, and a distinct well-funded segment will pay to have someone else operate it at scale.
**Source:** [Raj Dutt, SaaStr x Lightspeed talk; Sacra research aggregator](https://www.saastr.com/scaling-to-150m-arr-and-beyond-with-grafana-labs-and-lightspeed/)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `free-tier` `land-and-expand` `open-source` `word-of-mouth`

---

<a id="recipe-153"></a>

#### Free for one team, paid the moment it goes org-wide and production-critical

**The play:** Make the free core genuinely complete for a single team's main use case, then put the paywall exactly at what only an org running it in production needs: event/async protocols, SSO, multi-environment governance, alerting. Adoption stays frictionless and the price lands precisely where budget and urgency already exist.

**How [Gravitee](https://gravitee.io) did it** _(Reported)_ — Gravitee gives away the full core sync API gateway in the free Apache 2.0 Community Edition, then gates the features an enterprise architect needs to run it across an organization at scale: async/event-native protocols (Kafka, MQTT, TCP), enterprise SSO, multi-environment management via Cockpit, the Alert Engine, and advanced observability are Enterprise-Edition only. The free core seeds adoption inside a team; the moment the deployment becomes production-critical and event-driven, the paywall hits exactly where budget exists. That open-core line carried them to 200+ enterprise customers (Michelin, Roche, Eurostar) and ~$22M ARR.

**Result:** ~$22M ARR (FY24) at ~95% three-year average growth; 200+ enterprise customers; Gartner Magic Quadrant Leader for API Management (2024).
**The cost:** Pricing only the org-wide, production-critical and event-driven capabilities means a single team can run the free core indefinitely without converting, so revenue waits on a slow, sales-gated maturation cycle and a determined buyer can sometimes engineer around the enterprise boundary.
**When it works:** When there is a clean technical boundary between 'one team gets value' and 'the whole org runs it in production', and the second half is what only an enterprise budget will pay for.
**Source:** [VentureBeat coverage of Gravitee's open-core model; CE-vs-EE feature boundary confirmed via Gravitee docs (documentation.gravitee.io, OSS-vs-Enterprise feature comparison)](https://venturebeat.com/technology/open-source-api-management-platform-gravitee-raises-11m)
**Tags:** `freemium` `land-and-expand` `open-core` `open-source`

---

<a id="recipe-154"></a>

#### Make your paid product one env var away from your free framework

**The play:** Give away the OSS framework, then build the paid product as its default observability layer: wire it so an existing user turns on full tracing with one environment variable and an API key, no code change, project auto-created on first ingest. The gap between free and paid should be one line of config.

**How [LangChain](https://langchain.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — LangChain's MIT framework reached 95K+ GitHub stars and 90M+ monthly downloads, all free. They monetized with LangSmith, a SaaS observability/eval product wired so a LangChain or LangGraph user enables full tracing by setting a single environment variable (LANGSMITH_TRACING=true) plus an API key, with zero code changes and the tracing project auto-created on first ingest. The free framework became the distribution channel that walked developers into the paid product with near-zero setup friction. LangSmith hit 70K signups and 5K companies using it monthly within 7 months of its July 2023 launch, later 250K+ signups, 25K+ monthly active teams, and 1,000+ paying customers.

**Result:** 70K signups and 5K companies in 7 months; later 250K+ signups, 25K+ monthly active teams, 1,000+ paying customers, ~$12M-$16M ARR.
**The cost:** You give away the framework that 90M monthly downloads and 95K stars adopt for free, so monetization is confined to a thin observability layer the vast majority never pay for, leaving the ~$12M-$16M ARR a fraction of the install base and dependent on those users hitting production scale.
**When it works:** Works when you control a widely-adopted free framework or SDK that the paid product can hook into invisibly.
**Source:** [Contrary Research report on LangChain; single-env-var setup documented in LangChain's official tracing quickstart (docs.langchain.com/langsmith/observability-quickstart)](https://research.contrary.com/company/langchain)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `default-on` `free-tier` `land-and-expand` `open-source`

---

<a id="recipe-155"></a>

#### Make $5 your free tier, with a credit card on every signup

**The play:** After killing free, ship a near-zero paid floor ($5) that includes the full developer feature set, so the funnel stays wide while the card requirement filters out anyone who will never pay. Every signup arrives pre-qualified with a payment method attached.

**How [PlanetScale](https://planetscale.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — Killing free left PlanetScale without a low-end on-ramp for side projects and dev environments. In November 2025 it shipped a $5/month single-node Postgres plan that is production-ready and ships the full developer feature set (Query Insights, schema recommendations, branching, metrics), and cut development branches from $10 to $5. It positioned the tier explicitly for 'startups, side projects, proof of concepts, or development,' recapturing the low end as a paid, qualifying first step where every signup has a credit card attached, rather than free infrastructure.

**Result:** Created a $5/mo paid entry point with full feature access, restoring a low-friction funnel after the free tier was removed, with payment required from day one.
**The cost:** Putting a credit-card wall at the very bottom of the funnel forfeits the frictionless, free try-before-you-pay on-ramp that rivals like Neon and Supabase still offer, ceding casual-developer mindshare and the hobbyist adoption that often seeds future enterprise champions.
**When it works:** Works when your marginal cost per low-end user is non-trivial and serious builders will tolerate a card requirement.
**Source:** [PlanetScale blog ($5 PlanetScale is live, Nov 14 2025)](https://planetscale.com/blog/5-dollar-planetscale-is-here)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `land-and-expand` `pricing-change` `usage-based-pricing`

---

<a id="recipe-156"></a>

#### Make the trial generate the buyer's own ROI number

**The play:** If your product yields a quantifiable outcome (dollars saved, hours reclaimed), wire the trial into the prospect's real production data so the evaluation computes their personalized savings against their own infrastructure. The close becomes confirming a number they already watched accrue.

**How [Sedai](https://sedai.io) did it** _(Estimated)_ — Instead of demos and slides, Sedai's enterprise close is a POC where the platform connects to the prospect's real production environment and surfaces the actual dollars and engineering hours it would reclaim. The POC stops being a feature tour and becomes the business case: the prospect watches their own savings number accrue against their own infrastructure, so the buying decision is already proven before procurement starts.

**Result:** Sedai self-reports 7X revenue growth in 2024 and a 92% POC-to-customer conversion rate, citing Fortune 500 customers saving $5M+ annually and reclaiming 22,000+ engineering hours. (Both figures originate from Sedai's own Series B press release and are unverified.)
**The cost:** Wiring the trial into a prospect's real production cloud with read-write access forces a heavy, bespoke security and integration review per deal that doesn't scale, and hands the buyer a live kill switch the moment any savings number underwhelms.
**When it works:** Works when value is quantifiable per-customer and you can safely connect to live data; weak when the product needs months of usage before any value shows.
**Source:** [Sedai Series B announcement (June 16, 2025). The 7X and 92% figures are self-reported by Sedai and not independently verified.](https://sedai.io/blog/sedai-expands-its-self-driving-cloud-to-power-autonomous-enterprise-infrastructure-with-20m-series-b)
**Tags:** `design-partners` `founder-led-sales` `land-and-expand`

---

<a id="recipe-157"></a>

#### Hold the bottom of the market, because moving down later is far harder than moving up

**The play:** If your product spreads by one person carrying it to work, keep the free tier genuinely useful for a single user and delay enterprise features on purpose. Let the individual walk it into the org and convert there; you can always add upmarket features later, but you cannot recapture an abandoned base.

**How [Tailscale](https://tailscale.com) did it** _(Verified)_ — Tailscale committed to 'holding the bottom': a free personal plan an engineer adopts at home, then brings to a small team, then company-wide. Pennarun deliberately delayed enterprise features like device-posture checks, reasoning 'it's easy to move up market later, it's very hard to move down market later.' Enterprise demand then arrived organically, e.g. a bank CTO who first discovered Tailscale running on a friend's home Airbnb network. They enforced the free-tier device limit with a polite email asking 'would you mind paying us,' not a hard wall.

**Result:** Roughly half of MAU stay free and half convert to paid, an extraordinarily high split for devtools (typical is 2-5%). Reached ~20,000 paid business customers, doubling 5K -> 10K -> 20K every ~10 months with low sales touch.
**The cost:** By keeping the free tier genuinely useful and deliberately delaying enterprise features, you carry roughly half your users as non-paying load and forgo early enterprise ACV to win a base whose monetization arrives only later and on the user's timeline.
**When it works:** Works when a single individual gets real standalone value; fails when the product only delivers value at team or org scale, leaving the free tier hollow.
**Source:** [CEO Avery Pennarun, Stratechery interview (2025)](https://stratechery.com/2025/an-interview-with-tailscale-co-founder-and-ceo-avery-pennarun/)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `free-tier` `land-and-expand` `word-of-mouth`

---

<a id="recipe-158"></a>

#### Match the deposit to make them put money in first

**The play:** Run two offers side by side: a one-sided trial credit for tire-kickers, and a dollar-for-dollar deposit match that only unlocks once the user funds the account. The match self-selects committed buyers and pre-loads a balance they'll burn down, which converts to paying accounts far better than free credit alone.

**How [Vultr](https://vultr.com) did it** _(Verified)_ — As a pure self-serve cloud with no sales team, Vultr needed signups to convert into funded, retained accounts on their own. Its standing VULTRMATCH promo matches a new account's first deposit dollar-for-dollar up to $100, alongside larger one-sided trial credits ($200-$300). The match only pays out once the user puts their own money in, so it filters for real commitment instead of tire-kickers and turns a free trial into a funded, sticky balance the user is motivated to spend down.

**Result:** Standing acquisition offer; helped power self-serve growth to 1M+ customers and $125M ARR (Constant/Vultr, March 2022) with zero salespeople.
**The cost:** A dollar-for-dollar match is a direct margin subsidy on exactly the funded, high-intent accounts you most want, and running it as a standing promo trains every prospect to never deposit without claiming it, so the discount becomes a permanent cost of acquisition rather than a one-time nudge.
**When it works:** Works for usage-based, prepaid-balance products where a funded account predicts ongoing spend, and breaks for one-time or seat-based purchases where a balance has no pull.
**Source:** [Vultr coupons page (retrieved June 2026): 'VULTRMATCH - Double your deposit! Vultr will match your first deposit dollar for dollar, up to $100'; plus FLY300VULTR ($300), 250VULTRFLY ($250), FLYTWOHUNDRED ($200) new-customer credits.](https://www.vultr.com/coupons/)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `usage-based-pricing`

---

### Retention

<a id="recipe-159"></a>

#### Settle the outage in lock-in instead of refunds

**The play:** When you owe a make-good after an incident, pay it as credit toward a longer term and deeper module adoption, tied to a re-commitment, so the apology becomes a renewal that expands the account instead of a refund that resets it.

**How [CrowdStrike](https://crowdstrike.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — On July 19, 2024 a faulty CrowdStrike sensor update bricked 8.5M Windows machines worldwide and handed customers a credible reason to churn at renewal. Instead of writing refund checks, CrowdStrike offered 'customer commitment packages': discounts paired with subscription extensions and additional Falcon modules, structured so the make-good only paid out if the customer re-committed and signed a longer term. The compensation became a renewal and consolidation event that avoided a cash outflow, and gross retention barely moved.

**Result:** Gross retention held above 97% in the first full quarter after the outage, down less than half a percentage point. The packages carried an estimated ~$60M ARR/revenue impact across H2 FY2025 and, per CFO Burt Podbere, drove 'longer-term deals and increased adoption'; net retention stayed in the 112-115% range. CEO George Kurtz framed them as 'a proactive and concerted investment' to build loyalty.
**The cost:** Settling the make-good as discounts plus extensions and extra modules trains your largest, most-damaged customers to expect to be paid in deeper lock-in and lower effective prices, compressing margin and net retention on those accounts for the length of the longer terms you signed.
**When it works:** Works when you are an embedded system-of-record with high switching cost and a multi-product portfolio; it fails if a cheaper substitute exists or the make-good is so small relative to customer damages that it reads as insulting.
**Source:** [Cybersecurity Dive and AJC reporting on CrowdStrike Q3 FY2025 earnings; CFO Burt Podbere and CEO George Kurtz on-record quotes about 'customer commitment packages' (discounts, subscription extensions, additional offers) and the ~$60M ARR impact](https://www.cybersecuritydive.com/news/crowdstrike-retains-customers/734203/)
**Tags:** `system-of-record`

---

<a id="recipe-160"></a>

#### One shared data model turns every new product into a switching cost

**The play:** Ship and price each product separately, but inject shared identifiers (trace_id, service, env, host tags) so signals auto-correlate across them, making each product more useful and harder to unplug as customers add the next. Track the share of customers on 2+/4+/6+ products as your real retention leading indicator.

**How [Datadog](https://datadoghq.com) did it** _(Verified)_ — Datadog architected its agent to inject shared identifiers (trace_id, service, env, version, host tags) into every signal so metrics, traces, and logs auto-stitch into one investigation, instead of selling isolated tools. A log spike is only actionable when correlated to an APM trace tied to a deployment and a real-user session, so each product a team adds makes the others more valuable and harder to unplug. By Q4 2024, 83% of customers used 2+ products, 50% used 4+, 26% used 6+, and 12% used 8+, and that compounding adoption held gross revenue retention in the mid-to-high 90s.

**Result:** Gross revenue retention in the mid-to-high 90s and net revenue retention in the high 110s (Q4 2024); multi-product adoption climbing year-over-year (8+ products: 12% of customers, up from 9%). Customers using all three core pillars (infra, APM, logs) are reported to generate ~15x the revenue of single-product customers.
**The cost:** Forcing every product onto one shared data model and identifier scheme constrains each product to platform-wide architecture decisions and slows independent iteration, and the heavy unified-telemetry investment only pays off for multi-product customers while single-product users get the cost without the correlation payoff.
**When it works:** Works when your products operate on overlapping data that becomes more valuable when joined (observability, security, analytics) and customers expand product-by-product; it does little for standalone tools whose data has no cross-product join, where adding products just stacks unrelated invoices.
**Source:** [Olivier Pomel (CEO) and David Obstler (CFO), Datadog Q4 2024 earnings call: multi-product adoption percentages (83%/50%/26%/12%) and retention (GRR mid-to-high 90s, NRR high 110s). Unified-telemetry / shared-identifier architecture per Datadog product documentation and the 2019 S-1. The ~15x three-pillar revenue figure is from a secondary industry analysis (useluminix.com).](https://www.fool.com/earnings/call-transcripts/2025/02/13/datadog-ddog-q4-2024-earnings-call-transcript/)
**Tags:** `data-gravity` `land-and-expand` `switching-costs`

---

<a id="recipe-161"></a>

#### Three-year contracts let you lie to yourself about churn

**The play:** While the product is still maturing, price month-to-month even when investors push for annual lock-in, so stalled adoption surfaces in weeks instead of hiding for a year. Reinforce the loop by rotating every engineer through support, so the whole team feels churn directly.

**How [Datadog](https://datadoghq.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — Against investor pressure to lock in multi-year contracts, Datadog deliberately sold month-to-month early on. Pomel's logic: 'If we sell for three years, we won't have the bad news early enough. And we will be able to lie to ourselves by thinking the customer is not really using the product, but we're gonna fix it.' Monthly billing made stalled adoption and churn impossible to hide, and the feedback loop was reinforced by putting every engineer, not just support staff, on a one-week support rotation each year.

**Result:** Product gaps surfaced in weeks instead of being masked for a year, enabling the fast iteration that produced 146% net dollar retention at IPO.
**The cost:** You surrender the revenue predictability, contracted backlog, and forecastability that annual lock-in gives, which is exactly why Datadog's investors pushed against it, and you let every unhappy customer walk away at the end of any month.
**When it works:** Works when the product is still finding fit; once retention is proven, annual terms become worth the trade for predictable revenue.
**Source:** [Olivier Pomel (co-founder/CEO), via SaaStr 'The First $100,000,000 ARR at Datadog'](https://www.saastr.com/the-first-100000000-arr-at-datadog-how-founder-ceo-olivier-pomel-built-a-customer-centric-monitoring-giant/)
**Tags:** `pricing-change`

---

<a id="recipe-162"></a>

#### On a multi-module platform, shallow usage churns before low spend does

**The play:** Score retention risk by how many distinct use cases an account actively runs, with an explicit per-module threshold (GitLab flags accounts below 25% company-wide active usage as pilots). Hand CSMs a standard playbook to drive every healthy account into one more module before renewal.

**How [GitLab](https://gitlab.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — GitLab sells one platform spanning the whole DevOps lifecycle (plan, code, CI, secure, release, etc.), so a customer can pay yet only use one or two stages, leaving them easy to rip out. After a 2023 Sales Key Review, GitLab stood up a cross-functional Customer Use Case Adoption working group that named 'use case adoption' (how many lifecycle stages an account genuinely uses) its north-star KPI and #1 predictor of churn and contraction. CSMs now score each account on stages where >25% of the company is active (below 25% counts as a pilot, not adoption) and run 'Adoption Landing Zones' to push under-adopting customers into more stages before renewal.

**Result:** GitLab's handbook designates use case / stage adoption as the #1 (closed) predictor of churn and contraction; the company sustained dollar-based net retention of 120-130%+ while running this retention motion (DBNRR 122% as of Q1 FY2026).
**The cost:** A breadth-of-adoption north star only fits a sprawling suite and pushes CSMs to chase module-expansion checkboxes that can diverge from the single problem the customer actually came to solve.
**When it works:** Works when your product is a broad suite where customers can pay while using only a slice, so single-module accounts are the ones that churn; it adds little for single-purpose tools where there is no breadth to expand into.
**Source:** [GitLab public Handbook: Customer Use Case Adoption working group ('north star KPI and #1 predictor of Churn & Contraction', post 2023-05-16 Sales Key Review) and Stage Adoption Metrics page (>25% active-usage threshold); DBNRR from GitLab Q1 FY2026 IR release](https://handbook.gitlab.com/handbook/company/working-groups/customer-use-case-adoption/)
**Tags:** `land-and-expand` `net-retention`

---

<a id="recipe-163"></a>

#### Hard-code one opinionated workflow as the default

**The play:** Pick the single best way your customers should work and bake it in as a named, documented default (Triage, Cycles, fixed states); once a team's process lives inside your defaults, switching tools means re-inventing how they operate, which is what makes them stay.

**How [Linear](https://linear.app) did it** _(Verified)_ — Most B2B tools win deals by being infinitely configurable, which lets every team invent its own process and creates chaos at scale. Linear took the opposite bet: it built and published the 'Linear Method', baking one default way of working (Triage, Cycles, fixed issue states) directly into the product instead of offering a blank sandbox. Once a team runs its cadence on Linear's opinionated defaults, the tool stops being a task list and becomes how the team actually operates, so ripping it out means re-inventing the team's entire process. Linear reports 140%+ net revenue retention with near-zero paid marketing and a 4-person sales team serving 18,000+ paying companies.

**Result:** Net revenue retention 140%+ (CEO, Jan 2025); minimal churn cited across coverage; profitable since 2021 with ~0.5% of revenue spent on marketing.
**The cost:** Hard-coding one default way of working trades away every buyer whose process is idiosyncratic, regulated, or entrenched, capping your addressable market at teams willing to adopt your opinion and guaranteeing churn the instant your opinion conflicts with how a team must actually operate.
**When it works:** Works when you serve a workflow with a genuinely better 'right answer' and your buyers value focus over flexibility.
**Source:** [Linear official 'Linear Method' (principles/practices built into the product); NRR 140%+ from Karri Saarinen (CEO) on X, Jan 2025; opinionated-software framing corroborated by Figma blog 'The Linear Method: Opinionated Software'](https://linear.app/method)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg` `switching-costs`

---

<a id="recipe-164"></a>

#### Engineer the switching cost into their application code

**The play:** When you displace an incumbent, refuse the like-for-like swap: couple the customer's application logic to your model, then give away the migration tooling that makes that deeper rewrite cheap enough to commit to. Aim the lock-in at the application code itself, so leaving you means re-architecting the app.

**How [MongoDB](https://mongodb.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — When enterprises migrate off legacy relational databases, the easy sell is a like-for-like 'lift and shift' that swaps the database but leaves the application untouched, leaving you trivially replaceable. MongoDB CEO Dev Ittycheria instead pushes 'lift and transform': customers remodel their data into MongoDB's document model and rewrite the application's data-access layer against it, and MongoDB ships a free Relational Migrator to lower the cost of that rewrite. Once the application code itself is coupled to the document model, ripping MongoDB out forces a full application re-architecture, far beyond a table export, so customers standardize on it for mission-critical workloads and rarely leave.

**Result:** Net ARR expansion rate consistently above 120% (121% in Q4 FY2026, 128% at IPO); enterprises decommission legacy systems and standardize on MongoDB as a mission-critical platform, with one customer citing ~$50M in savings after consolidating onto it.
**The cost:** Forcing a 'lift and transform' makes every deal a funded application rewrite, a far longer and riskier sale than a drop-in swap, and it backfires into pure friction customers refuse whenever your model offers no genuine productivity gain over the incumbent.
**When it works:** Works when your product sits under an application and a genuinely different data/programming model gives real developer productivity gains, so the rewrite is justified on merit.
**Source:** [Dev Ittycheria (CEO), diginomica: 'I would more frame it as lift and transform' (vs lift-and-shift). Corroborated by diginomica 'mission-critical ready' interview ('customers truly view us as a strategic platform, and that's why you're seeing more and more customers standardize on MongoDB') and MongoDB's Relational Migrator product, which maps relational schemas to the document model and rewrites SQL data-access code. NRR figures from MongoDB FY2026 10-K / Q4 FY2026 results.](https://diginomica.com/dont-think-lift-and-shift-when-it-comes-cloud-migration-it-needs-be-lift-and-transform-says-mongodb)
**Tags:** `data-gravity` `enterprise-sales` `lock-in` `migration` `switching-costs` `system-of-record`

---

<a id="recipe-165"></a>

#### Optimize a recurring habit, and retention follows

**The play:** Pick the one repeatable core action users return to perform and that correlates with staying, measure it as unique active days within a 30-day window, rank every account by it, and aim onboarding and nudges at moving accounts up that ladder. The habit metric is the lever you pull; retention is the downstream result you stop chasing directly.

**How [Snyk](https://snyk.io) did it** _(Reported)_ — Snyk's growth team found acquisition was no longer the constraint, retention was, so they defined a single behavioral metric: the number of unique days an account fixed a vulnerability within 30 days. Plotting accounts into engagement states (Dormant, Casual, Core, Progressive) exposed a brutal split: dormant accounts had only a ~5-7% chance of still using Snyk twelve months later, while deeply engaged accounts retained at ~80%. They then re-pointed onboarding and product nudges at one job, getting accounts to fix vulnerabilities on more days, treating that recurring habit as the leading indicator of retention rather than tracking retention directly.

**Result:** Engagement state predicted 12-month retention with a >10x spread: ~5-7% for dormant accounts vs ~80% for deeply engaged accounts. Snyk reached >130% NRR (2022) and $300M+ ARR on this developer-habit foundation.
**The cost:** Optimizing everything around one recurring action (unique fix-days within 30 days) bakes in the assumption that frequency equals value, so you over-invest in nudging accounts up the engagement ladder and risk neglecting low-frequency-but-high-value users whose single use is the whole job.
**When it works:** Works when your product has a repeatable core action users return to perform (fix, deploy, query, ship), so frequency is a meaningful signal.
**Source:** [Ben Williams, 2x VP Product at Snyk (led PLG/growth), interviewed on Aakash Gupta's newsletter; corroborated by reo.dev citing his "Defining Activation at Snyk" essay (The Product-Led Geek)](https://www.news.aakashg.com/p/how-to-drive-growth-in-b2b-products)
**Tags:** `bottom-up-plg`

---

<a id="recipe-166"></a>

#### Your users already write the artifact; give them a one-click path to share it

**The play:** When users produce a reusable artifact while solving their own problem (rules, configs, integrations), run your library as a public repo and make upstreaming one step in the same format, so each customer's work compounds into product value for the next.

**How [Sublime Security](https://sublimesecurity.com) did it** _(Reported)_ — Instead of writing every detection in-house, Sublime ran its rule library like an open-source project on GitHub and let customers contribute the rules they wrote for attacks they were seeing in their own inboxes. CEO Josh Kamdjou's framing: 'Defenders know their networks better than anyone, but we weren't arming them as a community.' Because every user writes MQL rules for their own threats anyway, sharing them upstream is nearly free, and roughly one-third of the rules in the core feed now come from the community.

**Result:** ~1/3 of detection rules are community-contributed (Sublime authored ~2/3); community-authored rules have blocked tens of thousands of phishing attacks across the user base.
**The cost:** Leaning on community-contributed rules for a third of the feed means you inherit ongoing curation, quality-control, and security-review burden for code you did not write but are accountable for shipping to every customer.
**When it works:** Works when the artifact each user builds for themselves is generic enough to help the next user.
**Source:** [Josh Kamdjou (CEO) via TechCrunch (Feb 2023); one-third community-contributed rule stat](https://techcrunch.com/2023/02/21/sublime-nabs-9-8m-for-an-anti-phishing-email-security-platform-built-on-collective-crowdsourced-rules/)
**Tags:** `community-led` `developer-advocacy` `github` `open-source`

---

<a id="recipe-167"></a>

#### Every forced upgrade is a re-evaluation, and every re-evaluation is a chance to churn

**The play:** Publish an enumerated compatibility contract naming the exact APIs, file formats, client versions, and protocols you will never break, and engineer your data plane to keep routing and enforcing cached policy during your own control-plane outages. Then a customer never has a reason to reconsider whether to keep you.

**How [Tailscale](https://tailscale.com) did it** _(Verified)_ — Infrastructure that breaks on upgrade forces customers to constantly re-evaluate it, and every re-evaluation is a churn opportunity. Tailscale made an explicit public promise that the platform is 'backward compatible, forever': it will never break a client release people still use, never break a coordination-server API or policy-file syntax, and will support both IPv4 and IPv6 forever. CEO Avery Pennarun framed this as deliberately stepping off the industry's 'upgrade treadmill' so that once something works on Tailscale it keeps working with no maintenance and no surprises. The design extends to runtime: the network keeps routing traffic and enforcing cached access policies even when Tailscale's own coordination servers are down.
